Invalidity of Selling Milk in the Udder
Ruling that selling milk in the udder is invalid due to gharar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling milk in the udder and explain why it is invalid due to gharar.

Core Idea
Selling milk still in the udder is invalid because the quantity is unknown—uncertainty in the object of sale renders the contract fasid.

This short but powerful ruling illustrates a key Hanafi principle: any sale that involves uncertainty (gharar) regarding the subject matter is invalid. Here, the milk has not yet been milked, so its exact amount and quality are unknown, making the contract fasid. The proof comes from a hadith narrated by Ibn Abbas that explicitly prohibits selling 'milk in the udder'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Selling milk while it is still in the udder. The ruling is that this sale is not permissible (fasid).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof supports this ruling? The hadith from Ibn Abbas that includes 'or milk in the udder' as prohibited. This is a Sunnah ahad.
The full hadith text is cited in the previous lesson's footnote.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith specifically prove the ruling? The explicit mention of selling milk in the udder indicates prohibition. The underlying reason (illah) is gharar—uncertainty about the quantity and quality of the milk at the time of contract.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there any disagreement among schools? No, all schools agree on the impermissibility of selling milk in the udder due to gharar. It is muttafaq alayh.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? The sale is fasid, meaning it is not valid and does not transfer ownership. If the transaction occurs, the parties must undo it.

Source Text

This brief passage states the ruling that selling milk while it is still in the udder is not permissible. The reason, as given in the previous lessons and the shared hadith, is uncertainty (gharar) regarding the exact quantity and quality of the milk. The buyer cannot know how much milk will be extracted. This is included in the hadith from Ibn Abbas that also forbids selling wool on the back and ghee in milk. The sale is fasid.

Prohibition of Selling the Fetus in the Womb (Habl al-Habala)
Ruling and proof for prohibition of selling fetus and its offspring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a fetus in the womb and explain the dalil from the hadith of habl al-habala.

Core Idea
Selling a fetus or its unborn offspring is invalid because the object is completely unknown — a form of prohibited gharar.

This passage addresses a transaction that was practiced in pre-Islamic Arabia: selling a fetus still in the womb or even the offspring of that future fetus. The Hanafi school rules such sales invalid due to extreme uncertainty. Let's examine the ruling, its basis in the principle that the subject matter must be known, and the prophetic prohibition that serves as the primary proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Recognize that the text prohibits two things: selling a fetus in the womb and selling the offspring of that fetus. The ruling is fasid (invalid).
2
Understand the legal principle
The commentary states that the prohibition is based on the principle that the subject matter (mabi') is completely unknown (majhul). In Hanafi law, selling an unknown item is invalid.
This is a general principle: bay' al-majhul la yajuz.
3
Examine the primary proof (dalil)
The text cites a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar: the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of habl al-habala. Understand the historical context: the Jahiliyyah practice.
The hadith is authentic (Bukhari and Muslim).
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates that such a transaction is invalid (fasid) in the Hanafi school. The prohibition implies the absence of a valid contract.
Hanafi usul: al-nahy yufidu al-fasad fi al-uqud.
5
Identify the ikhtilaf and its thamarah
All schools prohibit gharar, but some may interpret the scope differently. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that any sale involving unknown future offspring is invalid.
The cross-madhhab dispute is a matter of definition, not core ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the sale of the fetus in the womb is not permissible, nor the sale of the offspring of the fetus. [885] (4) And it is not permissible to sell the fetus in the womb nor the offspring of the fetus. Yes, the fetus is in the womb and if one sells it, it is not permissible. Similarly, if one sells the offspring of the fetus that is in the womb, it is not permissible. (1) This merchandise is completely unknown; it is not known what the merchandise is like. Therefore, the sale is not permissible. This issue is based on the principle that if the merchandise is unknown, its sale is not permissible. (2) In the hadith, the Arabs of the pre-Islamic period used to sell the offspring of the fetus, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited it: from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of the offspring of the fetus (habl al-habala), which was a sale practiced by the people of the Jahiliyyah: a man would buy the camel until the she-camel gave birth, and then the one in its womb gave birth. (Bukhari, chapter of gharar sale and habl al-habala, p. 287, no. 2143; Muslim, chapter of prohibition of sale of habl al-habala, vol. 2, p. 2, no. 1514). In this hadith, selling the fetus and the offspring of the fetus is prohibited. Principle: The sale of unknown merchandise is invalid (fasid). Al-Khaj: the offspring of the fetus is called al-Fannaj. Footnote (a): The Prophet forbade the sale of the offspring of the fetus; the people of the Jahiliyyah used to practice such a sale. They used to sell in such a way that the buyer would buy the camel until the she-camel gave birth, and then when the offspring in its womb gave birth.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that it is not permissible to sell a fetus in the womb (حمل في البطن) nor the offspring of that fetus (نتاج/حبل الحبلة). The commentary explains that such a sale is invalid (فاسد) because the merchandise is completely unknown (مجہول). The ruling is based on the principle that selling an unknown item is not allowed. The proof is a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly forbade the sale of 'habl al-habala', which was a pre-Islamic practice where a person would buy a female camel on the condition that the buyer would receive the offspring of the offspring yet to be born. This sale involves multiple levels of uncertainty (gharar) and is therefore prohibited. The term 'حبل الحبلة' refers to the offspring of the fetus, which is completely unknown. The footnote adds further historical context, confirming the practice and prohibition.

Key Terms
حمل في البطن (haml fi al-batn): The fetus in the womb of an animal, sold separately from the mother.
حبل الحبلة (habl al-habala): The offspring of the fetus, i.e., the second generation of an unborn animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Selling a fetus or its offspring is invalid in Hanafi law due to complete uncertainty, based on the prophetic prohibition of habl al-habala.

Prohibition of Selling Wool on the Back of a Sheep
Ruling and proof for prohibition of selling wool still on the animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wool still on the animal, identify the evidence from hadith, and explain why uncertainty in quantity renders the sale invalid.

Core Idea
Selling wool still on a sheep is invalid because the exact quantity is unknown.

This lesson examines a specific prohibition in sales: selling wool that has not yet been shorn from the animal. The Hanafi school deems such a sale fasid due to uncertainty in the subject matter, relying on a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the general principle that merchandise must be clearly defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being sold (wool still on the animal) and state the ruling: the sale is fasid (invalid).
The ruling applies specifically to wool not yet separated from the animal.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence: the hadith of Ibn Abbas (RA) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling wool on the back, along with other examples.
The hadith is ahad but considered sahih and used as proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling: the explicit prohibition indicates that such a sale involves gharar (uncertainty) because the quantity of wool is unknown.
The prohibition is general and includes all cases of wool on the back.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may permit the sale if the wool is sold along with the animal, but the Hanafi position is that selling the wool alone is invalid.
The Hanafi view is mukhtalaf fih but mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the underlying principle
The sale is invalid because the limits (ḥudūd) of the merchandise are not determined, leading to jahalah (lack of specification).
This principle applies to all sales where the subject matter is uncertain.

And [it is not permissible to sell] wool on the back of sheep. [886] (5) And it is not permissible to sell wool on the back of a goat. Reason: The wool exists on the back of the goat or sheep, it has not yet been cut; in this state it is not permissible to sell it. This passage addresses the prohibition of selling wool while it is still on the back of a sheep or goat. The commentary explains the reason: the wool has not yet been cut, so the exact quantity and quality are unknown—there is uncertainty about how much will be cut and how much will remain. This lack of precise specification makes the sale invalid. The ruling is based on the principle that the limits (حدود) of the subject matter must be clearly defined in a sale. The footnote reinforces that such sales are prohibited because they involve selling items that are not yet separated or fully realized.

جہالت (jahalah): Lack of knowledge or specification about the subject matter of a contract.
حدود (hudud): The precise limits or specifications of the merchandise in a sale contract.

Prohibition of Selling Milk in the Udder
Ruling and proof for prohibition of selling milk in the udder, with a remedy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling milk in the udder, explain why it is invalid, and describe how it can become valid.

Core Idea
Milk in the udder is unknown and hidden, making its sale invalid unless extracted and re-contracted.

In this lesson, we examine a specific prohibition in sales: selling milk while it remains inside the animal's udder. The Hanafi school classifies this as an invalid sale (fasid) due to the milk being unknown in quantity and quality. However, the passage also provides a remedy: if the milk is first extracted and a new contract is made, the sale becomes valid. We will explore the legal reasoning and the supporting hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is selling milk that is still in the udder. The ruling is that it is not permissible (fasid).
The act is a sale (bai') of a specific object.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith 'aw laban fi dir'' (or milk in the udder) from Dar Qutni and Bayhaqi as proof.
This is a hadith ahad, but its chain is considered sound (thubut qati).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly forbids selling milk in the udder, indicating that the prohibition is due to gharar (uncertainty) because the milk is hidden and its quantity is unknown.
The forbidding language implies invalidity (fasid) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the remedy
The text states that if the milk is extracted (milked) and then a new contract is made, even tacitly through offer and acceptance, the sale becomes valid.
Extraction removes the uncertainty, making the milk known and measurable.
5
Evaluate the authority
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the core transmitted corpus of the Hanafi school. The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih (disputed among schools), but the Hanafi position is relied upon.
Some other schools may permit selling the milk with the animal, but Hanafis require extraction.

Source Text

And [it is not permissible to sell] milk in the udder. [887] (6) And it is not permissible to sell milk in the udder. The milk is in the udder, it has not yet been extracted, and he is selling it, so the milk is unknown and the nature is hidden, therefore its sale is not permissible. However, if he extracts it and then makes a new offer and acceptance tacitly, i.e., the seller gives and the buyer takes, then the sale becomes valid after being reversed. (2) The hadith has already passed in the previous issue: 'or milk in the udder' (Dar Qutni no. 281, Bayhaqi no. 10857). The sale of such unknown merchandise is not permissible. Al-Dir': udder.

Commentary
This passage deals with the prohibition of selling milk while it is still inside the udder. The commentary explains that the milk is unseen (مستور) and its exact quantity and quality are unknown (مجہول), making the sale invalid. However, there is an exception: if the milk is first extracted (milked) and then a new contract is made—even tacitly (سکوتی طور پر) through offer and acceptance (ایجاب و قبول)—the sale becomes valid. This is because after extraction, the milk becomes known and can be properly measured. The proof is the same hadith mentioned in the previous lesson, which includes the prohibition of selling milk in the udder along with other gharar sales. The term 'الضرع' means udder.

Key Terms
الضرع (al-dir'): The udder of an animal containing milk.
مستور (mastur): Hidden, not visible; referring to the milk being concealed inside the udder.

Selling Part of an Object That Causes Harm
Ruling and reasoning for prohibition of part-sales causing harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a part of an object that harms the seller's remaining property and apply the principle of 'la darar' to determine validity.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if separating the sold part harms the seller's remaining property.

Islamic law prohibits transactions that cause unjustified harm. This lesson examines a specific scenario: selling a portion of an object that, when separated, damages the seller's remaining property. The Hanafi school rules such sales invalid based on the prophetic maxim 'no harm and no reciprocating harm.' We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the exception when no harm occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Problem
Recognize that the sale involves part of an object that remains partly owned by the seller. The key issue is the effect of separation on the seller's remaining property.
2
State the General Rule
If separating the sold part causes harm to the seller's remaining property, the sale is invalid (fasid). The harm is unjustified because the seller does not consent to devaluation.
Harm can be physical damage or loss of utility.
3
Apply to the Examples
Examine the two cases: a yard cut from a cloth (making the remainder useless) and a beam removed from a roof (risk of collapse). Both cause harm, hence invalid.
The harm must be actual and not negligible.
4
Connect to the Dalil
The hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm) is the primary evidence. The sale here inflicts harm on the seller, so it is prohibited.
The hadith is a general principle in Islamic law.
5
Note the Exception
If separation does NOT harm the seller's property (e.g., modern cloth sold by the yard from a roll), the sale is permissible. The ruling is conditional on harm.
Contemporary practice often sells cloth in pre-cut pieces, so no harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز بيع ذراع من ثوب ولا بيع جذع من سقف [۸۸۸] (۷) اور نہیں جائز ہے گز کی بیع تھان میں سے اور نہ شہتیر کی بیع چھت میں سے۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ مبیع بائع کے مال کے ساتھ ملی ہوئی ہو اس لئے بیع کو اس سے الگ کرنے میں بائع کے مال کا نقصان ہوتا ہو۔ اب اس مبیع میں بائع کے مال کا بلا وجہ نقصان ہے اس لئے یہ بیع فاسد ہے۔ مثلا ایک گز کو تھان سے کاٹنے میں باقی تھان کا نقصان ہے کیونکہ وہ کسی کام کا نہیں رہے گا تو تھان میں سے ایک گز کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوگی۔ لیکن اگر گز کو الگ کرنے سے تھان کا نقصان نہیں ہے جیسا کہ اس زمانے میں ہوتا ہے تو ایک دو گز کی بیع جائز ہوگی۔ اسی طرح شہتیر چھت میں لگا ہوا ہے اس کو نکالنے سے پوری چھت کے گرنے کا یا کمزور ہونے کا خطرہ ہے تو ایسے شہتیر کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوگی۔ حدیث میں لا ضرر ولا ضرار ہے۔ (دار قطنی نمبر ۳۰۶۰) اس میں ہے کہ نہ نقصان دو اور نہ کسی سے نقصان اٹھاؤ۔ اور اس بیچ میں بائع کا نقصان ہے اس لئے بیع فاسد ہوگی۔ جذع : شہتیر جو چھت میں لگی ہوتی ہے اور ایک قسم کی لکڑی ہوتی ہے۔ سقف : چھت ۔
And it is not permissible to sell a yard from a cloth, nor to sell a beam from a roof. [888] (7) And it is not permissible to sell a yard from a cloth, nor to sell a beam from a roof. This issue is based on the principle that if the merchandise is mixed with the seller's property, and in separating it from that there is harm to the seller's property. Now in this merchandise, there is harm to the seller's property without reason, therefore this sale is invalid. For example, cutting a yard from a cloth harms the remaining cloth because it will become useless, so the sale of a yard from a cloth is not permissible. But if cutting the yard does not harm the cloth, as happens in this age, then selling one or two yards is permissible. Similarly, if a beam is fixed in the roof and removing it risks the entire roof collapsing or weakening, then such a beam's sale is not permissible. In the hadith: 'There is no harming and no reciprocating harm' (Dar Qutni no. 3060). It says: Do not cause harm nor let harm be caused to you. And in this sale, there is harm to the seller, therefore the sale is invalid. Jidh': a beam that is fixed in the roof and is a type of wood. Saqf: roof.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of selling a part of an object that, if separated, would cause harm to the remaining part owned by the seller. The commentary explains the principle: if the subject matter is intermixed with the seller's property and its extraction causes damage to the seller's property without a valid reason, the sale is invalid. The example given is selling a single yard (ذراع) from a cloth; cutting it would ruin the rest of the cloth, making it useless. However, if cutting does not cause harm (as in modern times where cloth is sold in pre-cut pieces), then it becomes permissible. Similarly, selling a beam (جذع) from a roof is prohibited because removing it may cause the roof to collapse or weaken. The proof is the hadith 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' (no harm and no reciprocating harm), which is a general principle in Islamic law. The commentary uses this to argue that any transaction causing unjustified harm to the seller is invalid.

Key Terms
ذراع من ثوب (dhira' min thawb): A yard (specific length) sold from a larger piece of cloth.
جذع من سقف (jidh' min saqf): A beam that is part of a roof structure.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A hadith stating that no harm should be inflicted nor harm reciprocated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Selling part of an object that damages the seller's remaining property is invalid because of the prohibition of harm in Islamic law.

Prohibition of Selling a Throw of a Net (Darbat al-Gha'is)
Ruling and proof for prohibition of selling an uncertain catch from a net dive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for darbat al-gha'is and identify the hadith evidence that prohibits it.

Core Idea
A sale of unknown catch from a single net throw is invalid due to gharar.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, a sale where the subject matter is completely unknown is invalid. One such example is 'darbat al-gha'is' — selling whatever a diver catches in a single net throw. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale where a fixed price is paid for whatever fish is caught in one net throw. The ruling is that it is not permissible (fasid).
The uncertainty (gharar) is in the quantity and existence of the fish.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: the Prophet forbade buying the throw of the diver. Also the general prohibition of gharar sales from Abu Huraira.
These are ahad hadith, but thubut is qati due to wide acceptance.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition indicates that such a transaction is invalid. The word 'gharar' implies deception and uncertainty, which are the core reasons for prohibition.
The Hanafi school considers gharar sales as fasid, not batil, because the defect is in the description, not the essence.
4
Derive the thamarah (practical consequence)
If such a sale occurs, it is fasid. The buyer has the option to cancel or to pay the market price of what he actually received.
The sale is not completely void but defective; it can be rectified with mutual consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضربة الغائص [۸۸۹] (۸) اور نہیں جائز ہے جال کا ایک پھینک۔ یوں کہا کہ ایک مرتبہ پانی میں جال پھینکتا ہوں اس میں جتنی مچھلی آجائے اس کی قیمت مثلا پانچ پونڈ ہوگی تو اس طرح کی بیع جائز نہیں اس میں مبیع مجہول ہے معلوم نہیں کتنی مچھلی آئے گی اور نہیں آئے گی۔ اور یہ بھی یہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ تھوڑی سی مچھلی آئے اور مفت میں پانچ پونڈ دینا پڑے اس لئے یہ بیع جائز نہیں عن ابي هرير قال نهى رسول الله الا الله عن بيع الغرر وبيع الحصاة (الف) (ترندى شریف، باب ماجاء فی کراہیتہ بیع الخروص ۲۳۲ نمبر ۱۲۳۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ دھوکے کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے۔ عن ابی سعید الخدري قال رسول الله الا الله ... وعن شراء ضربة الغائص (دار قطني، كتاب البيوع ج ثالث ص ۱۲ نمبر ۲۸۱۵) اس حدیث میں ضربتہ الغائص کو باضابطہ منع فرمایا ہے۔ اسوال جس بیچ میں دھو کہ ہو وہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے دھوکہ کی بیچ سے روکا اور کنکری مارنے کی بیچ سے روکا۔
And the throw of the diver (net). [889] (8) And it is not permissible to sell a throw of a net. That is, to say: I will throw the net once into the water, whatever fish comes in it, its price will be, for example, five pounds. Such a sale is not permissible. In it, the merchandise is unknown; it is not known how much fish will come or not come. And it is also possible that very few fish come and he has to give five pounds for nothing. Therefore, this sale is not permissible. From Abu Huraira: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of gharar (deception) and the sale of pebbles. (Tirmidhi, chapter on the dislike of the sale of fruits, no. 1230). From this hadith, it is known that a deceptive sale is not permissible. From Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: '...and from buying the throw of the diver.' (Dar Qutni, book of sales, vol. 3, p. 12, no. 2815). In this hadith, the throw of the diver is explicitly prohibited. Conclusion: Any sale that involves deception (gharar) is not permissible. Footnote (a): The Prophet forbade the sale of deception and the sale of pebbles.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of a sale known as 'ضربة الغائص' (the throw of the diver), which refers to selling the catch from a single toss of a fishing net. The commentary explains that this is invalid because the merchandise (the fish) is completely unknown (مجہول); neither the quantity nor even whether any fish will be caught is known. The buyer might pay a fixed price (e.g., five pounds) and receive nothing or very little, which is a form of gharar (deception). The proof is from two hadiths: first, the general prohibition of gharar sales (بيع الغرر) from Abu Huraira in Tirmidhi, and second, the specific prohibition of buying the throw of the diver from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri in Dar Qutni. The footnote adds that the Prophet also forbade the sale of pebbles (بيع الحصاة), another type of gharar. The underlying principle is that any sale involving uncertainty or deception is not permissible in Islamic law.

Key Terms
ضربة الغائص (darbat al-gha'is): A sale where the buyer pays a fixed price for whatever the diver catches in a single dive or net throw.
بيع الغرر (bay' al-gharar): A sale involving deception, uncertainty, or risk about the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which hadith specifically prohibits the sale of 'ضربة الغائص' (throw of the diver)?
A) The hadith of Abu Huraira about gharar
B) The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri
C) The hadith of Ibn Abbas about fruit
D) The hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar about habl al-habala

Key Takeaway
The sale of an uncertain catch from a single net throw is prohibited because it involves gharar (deception), and the Prophet explicitly forbade it.

Prohibition of Selling the Catch of a Single Net Throw (Bay' Darbat al-Gha'is)
Ruling on selling the catch of a single net throw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a diver's plunge, explain why it constitutes gharar, and cite the relevant hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Selling an unknown catch from a single net throw is batil due to gharar.

This lesson explores a classic example of prohibited sales in Hanafi fiqh: selling the catch of a single net throw. The transaction appears straightforward, but the uncertainty about what will be caught makes it invalid. We will examine the reasoning, the prophetic prohibition, and why this ruling remains relevant today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? (The catch of a single net throw.) What is the hukm? (It is batil, i.e., invalid.)
The ruling is clear: such a sale is not permissible.
2
Pinpoint the cause of prohibition
Why is it invalid? Because the sold item (the fish) is unknown (majhul) in quantity and existence. This is gharar (uncertainty/deception).
Uncertainty in the essential terms of a sale invalidates it.
3
Analyze the dalil evidence
The text cites two hadith: one general prohibition of gharar and hasah, and one specific to buying a diver's plunge. Understand how they prove the ruling.
The hadith are ahad (zanni thubut) but qati dalala (clear meaning).
4
Confirm the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds this sale as batil (void). There is no ikhtilaf among the major imams on this point.
The ruling is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon).

And it is not permissible to sell a net throw. For example, one says: 'I throw the net once into the water, and whatever fish comes, its price will be five pounds.' Such a sale is not permissible. In it, the sold item is unknown; it is not known how many fish will come or not. Also, it may happen that few fish come and he has to pay five pounds for free. Therefore, this sale is not permissible. From Abu Huraira: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of gharar and the sale of hasah. (Tirmidhi, chapter on dislike of selling fruits, no. 1230). This hadith indicates that a deceptive sale is not permissible. From Abu Sa'eed al-Khudri: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade buying the plunge of a diver. (Darqutni, book of sales, part 3, p. 12, no. 2815). In this hadith, the plunge of a diver is explicitly prohibited. Therefore, any transaction involving deception is not permissible. Footnote (a): He forbade deceptive sale and the sale of hasah.

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling the catch of a single net throw, known as bay' darbat al-gha'is (sale of a diver's plunge). The text explains that such a sale is impermissible because the sold item (the fish) is unknown (majhul). The buyer pays a fixed price for whatever fish are caught, but the quantity is uncertain. This constitutes gharar (deception/uncertainty), which is explicitly prohibited by the Prophet (peace be upon him) in hadith. The text cites two hadith: one from Abu Huraira forbidding bay' al-gharar and bay' al-hasah, and another from Abu Sa'eed al-Khudri specifically forbidding شراء ضربة الغائص (buying a diver's plunge). The footnote explains that gharar is deceptive sale. Thus, the ruling is that any sale involving uncertainty is invalid. This is a clear ruling from the Hanafi school, based on prophetic prohibition.

Key Terms
ضربة الغائص (darbat al-gha'is): The plunge of a diver; here meaning a single throw of a fishing net, the result of which is uncertain.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or deception in a transaction; specifically, the sold item's quantity, quality, or existence is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Bay' al-Muzabanah and the Exception of 'Araya
Ruling on selling fresh dates on tree for dried dates by estimation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on muzabanah, identify its proof, and distinguish the Hanafi interpretation of 'araya from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is prohibited due to riba and gharar; the 'araya exception is a gift, not a sale.

This passage addresses the sale of fresh dates on the tree for dried dates by estimation, known as bay' al-muzabanah. While the Hanafi school holds it invalid due to riba and gharar, Imam al-Shafi'i permits it in limited quantities based on the concession of 'araya. We will explore the proofs and the Hanafi response that reinterprets 'araya as a gift transaction.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Muzabanah is invalid; the hadith of Abu Sa'eed al-Khudri explicitly forbids it.
The prohibition is based on the potential inequality in exchange of the same genus.
2
Identify the opposing school's view and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i permits muzabanah for quantities under five wasqs, relying on the hadith of 'araya.
The Shafi'i view treats 'araya as a genuine sale.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that 'araya is not a sale but a gift exchange, so the original prohibition remains.
They cite the interpretation of Sufyan ibn Husain recorded by Bukhari.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: any muzabanah transaction is invalid; Shafi'i: valid if less than five wasqs.
This affects how scholars advise on similar exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۸۹۰] (۹) ولا بيع المزابنة وهو بيع التمر على النخل بخرصة تمرا.
الغائص : غوطہ لگانے والا ۔
[۸۹۰] (۹) اور نہیں جائز ہے بیچ مزابنہ ، وہ یہ ہے کہ کھجور کی بیع کھجور کے درخت پر ٹوٹے ہوئے کھجور سے اندازہ کر کے۔
کھجور کے درخت پر کھجور لگا ہوا ہو اس کو ٹوٹے ہوئے کھجور کے بدلے میں بیچے تو یہ بیع فاسد ہے۔ (1) ادھر بھی کھجور ہے اور درخت پر بھی کھجور ہے لیکن درخت پر کھجور کتنا ہے اس کا اندازہ نہیں ہے اس لئے کھجور کے بدلے میں کھجور کمی بیشی ہو گی تو ر ہوا اور سود ہو جائے گا اس لئے جائز نہیں ہے (۲) مزابنہ کے نا جائز ہونے کی یہ دلیل یہ حدث ہے عن ابی سعید الخدری ان رســول اللـه نهى عن المزانبة والمحاقلة ، والمزابنة اشتراء التمر بالتمر على رؤوس النخل (الف) ( بخاری شریف، بع المز النبتة وهی بیع التمر بالشمر ص ۲۹۱ نمبر ۲۱۸۶ / مسلم شریف، باب النھی عن المحاقلة والمز ابنته ج ثانی ص۱۰ نمبر ۱۵۳۹) اس حدیث میں مزانیہ کو حضور نے منع فرمایا ہے۔
نا امام شافعی کے نزدیک پانچ وسق سے کم میں جائز ہے۔
کیونکہ عرایا میں حضور نے اجازت دی ہے۔ اور عرایا کی شکل یہ ہے کہ ٹوٹے ہوئے کھجور کے بدلے درخت پر لگے ہوئے کھجور کو خریدے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن ابي هريرة ان النبى الله ورخص في بيع العرايا في خمسة اوسق او دون خمسة اوسق قال نعم (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب بیع التمر على رؤوس النخل بالذهب والفضة ص ۲۹۱ نمبر ۱۲۱۹ مسلم شریف، باب تحریم بیع الرطب بالتمر الا فی العرايا ص ۸ نمبر (۱۵۴) اس حدیث میں آپ نے عرایا کی بیچ کرنے کی اجازت دی اگر پانچ وسق سے کم ہو، اور بعض حدیث میں ہے کہ ایک دو
درخت ہو تو جائز ہے۔ حنفیہ کے نزدیک عرایا اصل میں درخت کے مالک کی جانب سے ہدیہ ہے بیچ نہیں ہے۔ صرف بیع کی صورت ہے۔ اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اہل عرب مساکین کو ایک دو درخت کھانے کے لئے ہدیہ دے دیا کرتے تھے۔ لیکن غربت کی وجہ سے وہ کھجور پکنے تک صبر نہیں کر پاتے تو اس درخت کے کھجور کے بدلے مالک درخت سے کٹے ہوئے کھجور دے دیا کرتے تھے۔ جو صورت میں بیج ہے لیکن حقیقت میں پہلے والا ہدیہ ہی کئے ہوئے کھجور کی صورت میں دینا ہے۔ خود امام بخاری نے سفیان بن حسین کے واسطے سے عرایا کی یہی تفسیر بیان کی ہے۔ عبارت یہ ہے عن سفيان بن حسين العرايا نخل كانت توهب للمساكين فلا يستطيعون ان ينتظروا بها فرخص لهم ان يبيعواها بما شاء وا من التمر (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب تفسیر العرايا ص ۲۹۲ نمبر ۲۱۹۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہدیہ کے بدلے درخت کا مالک ہدیہ دے رہا ہے یہی عرایا ہے ۔ اور اس کی گنجائش ہمارے یہاں بھی ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے مزانبہ اور حاقلہ کی بیچ سے روکا، مزانہ کی تغییر یہ ہے کہ کھجور کے درخت پر کھجور ہو اس کے بدلے میں کھجور خریدے (ب) آپ نے عرایا کی بیچ میں رخصت دی پانچ وسق یا اس سے کم میں فرمایا ہاں (ج) سفیان بن حسین نے فرمایا عرایا کھجور کا درخت ہوتا ہے۔ اس کو مساکین کو ہبہ کردے پھر وہ انتظار نہ کر سکے تو ان مسکینوں کے لئے رخصت ہے کہ اس کو جیتنے کھجور میں چاہے بیچے۔
And not permissible is the sale of muzabanah, which is the sale of dates on the palm tree for an estimated measure of dates. Al-Gha'is: one who dives. (9) And it is not permissible to sell muzabanah, that is, to sell dates on the tree for an estimated amount of dried dates. If dates are on the tree and one sells them for dried dates by estimation, this sale is invalid. (1) Because both sides involve dates but the quantity on the tree is unknown, so there may be excess or deficiency, resulting in riba (interest). Therefore, it is not permissible. (2) The proof for the impermissibility of muzabanah is the hadith: from Abu Sa'eed al-Khudri that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade muzabanah and muhaqalah, and muzabanah is buying dates on the tree for dates. (Bukhari, Muslim). This hadith prohibits muzabanah. Now, according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is permissible if less than five wasqs, because the Prophet allowed 'araya. 'Araya is buying dates on the tree for dried dates. The hadith: from Abu Huraira that the Prophet gave a concession for 'araya in five wasqs or less. (Bukhari, Muslim). So the Prophet permitted it if less than five wasqs, or some narrations say one or two trees. According to the Hanafis, 'araya is essentially a gift from the tree owner, not a sale. It takes the form of a sale, but the reason is that the Arabs used to give one or two trees as a gift to the poor. Due to poverty, the poor could not wait for the dates to ripen, so they would give dried dates in exchange for the dates on the tree. This is a sale in form but a gift in reality. Imam Bukhari himself, through Sufyan ibn Husain, explained 'araya this way: 'Araya are palm trees that were given as gifts to the poor, and they were allowed to sell them for whatever dates they wished.' This effect shows that the owner is giving a gift in exchange for a gift; that is 'araya. And this concession is also valid in our school. Footnotes: (a) He forbade muzabanah and muhaqalah; muzabanah is buying dates on the tree for dates. (b) He gave concession for 'araya for five wasqs or less, he said yes. (c) Sufyan ibn Husain said: 'Araya are palm trees given as gifts to the poor; then they cannot wait, so they are allowed to sell them for whatever dates they wish.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on bay' al-muzabanah, defined as the sale of fresh dates on the tree for dried dates by estimation. The text declares it invalid (fasid) because it involves riba: since the quantity on the tree is unknown, there is likely inequality in exchange, which is prohibited. The primary proof is the hadith of Abu Sa'eed al-Khudri explicitly forbidding muzabanah. Then the text presents a difference of opinion with Imam al-Shafi'i, who permits it when less than five wasqs based on the concession for 'araya (a specific type of transaction). The Hanafi response is that 'araya is not a true sale but a gift in form: the owner gives the trees as a gift to the poor, and the poor, unable to wait, exchange them for dried dates. This is a gift being exchanged for a gift, not a sale, so the ruling on muzabanah remains. The Hanafis rely on the interpretation of Sufyan ibn Husain transmitted by Bukhari. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains the general prohibition of muzabanah, with the exception of 'araya only under its specific interpretation.

Key Terms
مزابنة (muzabanah): A sale of fresh dates on the tree for a measured amount of dried dates by estimation, prohibited due to riba and gharar.
عرايا ('araya (sing. 'ariyyah)): Palm trees given as gifts to the poor, who then exchange the fresh dates for dried dates out of necessity.
وسق (wasq): A measure of volume, equivalent to approximately 60 sa' (about 130 kg for dates).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the buyer does not have possession
D) Because it is a type of gambling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains the prohibition of muzabanah due to riba, interpreting the 'araya concession as a gift exchange rather than a sale.

Prohibition of Muzabanah and the 'Araya Dispute
States ruling on muzabanah and discusses ikhtilaf with Shafi'i on 'araya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muzabanah, explain the illa of prohibition, and contrast the Shafi'i position on 'araya with the Hanafi rebuttal.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is prohibited due to riba; the 'araya exception is reinterpreted by Hanafis as a gift, not a sale.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Samari presents the classical Hanafi ruling on muzabanah—selling dates on the tree for dried dates by estimation—and immediately engages the Shafi'i position on 'araya. The text grounds the prohibition in a prophetic hadith and then responds to the opposing school's proof, demonstrating how the Hanafi school preserves the general riba principle while accommodating a common practice through legal recharacterization. You will learn to distinguish a prohibited sale from a permissible gift form.

Guided Path
1
Define Muzabanah
Identify the exact definition given in the text: selling dates on the palm tree for dried dates by estimation (kharṣah). Understand that the essence is an exchange of dates for dates with unknown equivalence.
The uncertainty (jahl) is the key illa.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling and its Proof
From the text, extract that muzabanah is not permissible (la yajuz) and note the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri forbidding it. Recognize the prohibition as explicit (nahy) indicating haram.
The hadith is the primary dalil.
3
Present the Shafi'i Position
Identify that Imam al-Shafi'i permits a limited form of this transaction for up to five wasaq, based on the hadith of 'araya (permission for poor recipients to sell gifted dates for dried dates).
This is the opposing proof.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Understand that Hanafis reinterpret 'araya as a gift (hibah), not a sale, citing the interpretation of Sufyan ibn Husain recorded by Bukhari. The transaction is merely a convenience for the poor, not a genuine sale of dates for dates.
The form may resemble a sale, but the substance is a gift.
5
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Conclude that the practical difference is: Hanafis forbid any sale of dates for dates with known inequality regardless of quantity; Shafi'is allow it up to five wasaq when motivated by charity. Hanafis maintain the prohibition without exception, recharacterizing the permitted case as a non-sale.
Understanding this difference clarifies why the schools diverge.

کھجور کے درخت پر کھجور لگا ہوا ہو اس کو ٹوٹے ہوئے کھجور کے بدلے میں بیچے تو یہ بیع فاسد ہے۔ (1) ادھر بھی کھجور ہے اور درخت پر بھی کھجور ہے لیکن درخت پر کھجور کتنا ہے اس کا اندازہ نہیں ہے اس لئے کھجور کے بدلے میں کھجور کمی بیشی ہو گی تو ر ہوا اور سود ہو جائے گا اس لئے جائز نہیں ہے (۲) مزابنہ کے نا جائز ہونے کی یہ دلیل یہ حدث ہے عن ابی سعید الخدری ان رســول اللـه نهى عن المزانبة والمحاقلة ، والمزابنة اشتراء التمر بالتمر على رؤوس النخل (الف) ( بخاری شریف، بع المز النبتة وهی بیع التمر بالشمر ص ۲۹۱ نمبر ۲۱۸۶ / مسلم شریف، باب النھی عن المحاقلة والمز ابنته ج ثانی ص۱۰ نمبر ۱۵۳۹) اس حدیث میں مزانیہ کو حضور نے منع فرمایا ہے۔
نا امام شافعی کے نزدیک پانچ وسق سے کم میں جائز ہے۔
کیونکہ عرایا میں حضور نے اجازت دی ہے۔ اور عرایا کی شکل یہ ہے کہ ٹوٹے ہوئے کھجور کے بدلے درخت پر لگے ہوئے کھجور کو خریدے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن ابي هريرة ان النبى الله ورخص في بيع العرايا في خمسة اوسق او دون خمسة اوسق قال نعم (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب بیع التمر على رؤوس النخل بالذهب والفضة ص ۲۹۱ نمبر ۱۲۱۹ مسلم شریف، باب تحریم بیع الرطب بالتمر الا فی العرايا ص ۸ نمبر (۱۵۴) اس حدیث میں آپ نے عرایا کی بیچ کرنے کی اجازت دی اگر پانچ وسق سے کم ہو، اور بعض حدیث میں ہے کہ ایک دو
درخت ہو تو جائز ہے۔ حنفیہ کے نزدیک عرایا اصل میں درخت کے مالک کی جانب سے ہدیہ ہے بیچ نہیں ہے۔ صرف بیع کی صورت ہے۔ اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اہل عرب مساکین کو ایک دو درخت کھانے کے لئے ہدیہ دے دیا کرتے تھے۔ لیکن غربت کی وجہ سے وہ کھجور پکنے تک صبر نہیں کر پاتے تو اس درخت کے کھجور کے بدلے مالک درخت سے کٹے ہوئے کھجور دے دیا کرتے تھے۔ جو صورت میں بیج ہے لیکن حقیقت میں پہلے والا ہدیہ ہی کئے ہوئے کھجور کی صورت میں دینا ہے۔ خود امام بخاری نے سفیان بن حسین کے واسطے سے عرایا کی یہی تفسیر بیان کی ہے۔ عبارت یہ ہے عن سفیان بن حسين العرايا نخل كانت توهب للمساكين فلا يستطيعون ان ينتظروا بها فرخص لهم ان يبيعواها بما شاء وا من التمر (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب تفسیر العرايا ص ۲۹۲ نمبر ۲۱۹۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہدیہ کے بدلے درخت کا مالک ہدیہ دے رہا ہے یہی عرایا ہے ۔ اور اس کی گنجائش ہمارے یہاں بھی ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے مزانبہ اور حاقلہ کی بیچ سے روکا، مزانہ کی تغییر یہ ہے کہ کھجور کے درخت پر کھجور ہو اس کے بدلے میں کھجور خریدے (ب) آپ نے عرایا کی بیچ میں رخصت دی پانچ وسق یا اس سے کم میں فرمایا ہاں (ج) سفیان بن حسین نے فرمایا عرایا کھجور کا درخت ہوتا ہے۔ اس کو مساکین کو ہبہ کردے پھر وہ انتظار نہ کر سکے تو ان مسکینوں کے لئے رخصت ہے کہ اس کو جیتنے کھجور میں چاہے بیچے۔
The commentary: Volume 2, page 48, Book of Sales. [890] (9) And there is no valid sale of muzabanah, which is selling dates on the palm trees for dried dates by estimation. Q: الغائص means one who dives. [890] (9) And it is not permissible to sell muzabanah, which is that dates are sold on the date palm in exchange for dried dates by estimation. There are dates on the palm tree, and he sells them in exchange for dried dates—this sale is invalid (fasid). (1) Here there are dates, and on the tree there are also dates, but the amount on the tree is not known, so exchanging dates for dates involves excess and deficiency, leading to riba, so it is not permissible. (2) The evidence for the impermissibility of muzabanah is the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Messenger of Allah forbade muzabanah and muhadhalah. Muzabanah is purchasing dates for dates on the heads of palm trees. (Reference: Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, the Prophet prohibited muzabanah. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is permissible for less than five wasaq, because the Prophet allowed 'araya. The form of 'araya is that one purchases dates on the tree in exchange for dried dates. There is a hadith from Abu Hurairah that the Prophet permitted the sale of 'araya for five wasaq or less. (Reference: Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, the Prophet gave permission for the sale of 'araya if it is less than five wasaq, and some hadith say one or two trees are permissible. According to the Hanafis, 'araya is originally a gift from the owner of the tree, not a sale; it only takes the form of a sale. The reason is that the Arabs used to give one or two trees as a gift to the poor to eat, but due to poverty they could not wait for the dates to ripen, so the owner of the tree would give them cut dates in exchange for the dates on the tree. This takes the form of a sale, but in reality it is giving the previous gift in the form of dates. Imam Bukhari himself narrated from Sufyan ibn Husain the same interpretation of 'araya. (Reference: Bukhari) From this effect, it is understood that the owner of the tree is giving a gift in exchange for a gift—this is 'araya. And there is leniency for this among us as well. Footnote: (a) He forbade selling muzabanah and muhadhalah. The explanation of muzabanah is that there are dates on the palm tree, and he buys them in exchange for dates. (b) He permitted the sale of 'araya for five wasaq or less; he said yes. (c) Sufyan ibn Husain said: 'Araya are date palms that were given as a gift to the poor, then they could not wait for them, so they are permitted to sell them for as many dates as they wish.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage from Al Sharh Ul Sameri begins the Book of Sales with a discussion of prohibited transactions. The first ruling is on muzabanah, defined as selling dates still on the tree for a fixed amount of dried dates by estimation. The author states it is not permissible (la yajuz) because it involves indeterminacy (jahl) and potential riba (usury) when exchanging homogenous goods (dates for dates) with unknown equivalence. The proof is a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri where the Prophet explicitly forbade muzabanah and muhadhalah. The passage then presents an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i permits a limited form of this transaction—selling dates on the tree for dried dates up to five wasaq (a measure)—based on the hadith of 'araya (a special permission). The Hanafi response, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, is that 'araya is not a true sale but a gift (hibah) from the owner to the poor, which is then compensated with dried dates as a convenience. The Hanafis cite an interpretation from Sufyan ibn Husain recorded by Bukhari to support this view. The key point is that for Hanafis, any sale of dates for dates with inequality is prohibited due to the general rule of riba in homogenous weighable/measurable items, regardless of quantity. The footnote clarifies the hadith texts further. This lesson demonstrates how a textual proof (hadith) and a rational principle (prohibition of riba) are applied, and how different schools interpret exceptional permissions.

Key Terms
المزابنة (al-muzabanah): Selling dates on the palm tree for a fixed amount of dried dates by estimation, involving uncertainty and potential riba.
العرايا (al-'araya): A transaction where the owner gives a few date palms to the poor, and later the poor can sell the dates on the tree for dried dates as a concession.
خرصة (kharṣah): Estimation of the quantity of dates on the tree to determine the exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is muzabanah prohibited even if the amount exchanged is small, like less than five wasaq?
A) Because it involves a delay in delivery.
B) Because it involves uncertainty and potential riba when exchanging dates for dates.
C) Because the hadith of 'araya only permits it for two trees.
D) Because it is a sale of fruit before it ripens.

Key Takeaway
Muzabanah is prohibited due to riba; Hanafis do not accept the 'araya exception as a true sale but treat it as a gift.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Prohibition of Sales with Uncertainty: Throwing Stone, Touching, and Unspecified Cloth
Prohibits sales with uncertainty: throwing a stone, touching, and two cloths without specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-hajar, al-malamasah, and bay' al-thawb min al-thawbayn, and identify the shared illa (gharar) that makes them invalid.

Core Idea
Sales where the object or consent is uncertain are haram.

Sales from the Jahiliyyah often involved deception and lack of genuine agreement. The Hanafi school condemns three specific forms: throwing a stone to determine the item, touching making the sale binding, and picking one of two cloths without specification. This lesson unpacks the fiqh reasoning and the hadith proof that underlies these prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibited sales
List them: bay' al-hajar (throwing stone), bay' al-malamasah (touching), and bay' al-thawb min al-thawbayn (one of two cloths). Understand the pre-Islamic practices behind each.
These are not hypothetical; they were actual practices.
2
Recognize the common illa (effective cause)
All three involve gharar (uncertainty) about the object of sale, price, or consent. Gharar invalidates the contract because the parties do not genuinely agree.
Gharar can be excess or deficiency, but here it is fundamental.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri explicitly forbids munabadhah and malamasah. Understand the text and how it covers bay' al-hajar by analogy.
The Prophet ﷺ prohibited these as they lead to disputes.
4
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Any sale where the buyer cannot inspect the item or where the contract is forced by chance (e.g., mystery boxes, blind auctions) falls under this prohibition.
The illa of gharar is timeless.

Source Text

[891] (10) And it is not permissible to sell by throwing a stone or by touching. [892] (11) And it is not permissible to sell one of two cloths. Imam Abu Hanifa's view turned to this point: selling dates in exchange for dates is riba, so it is not permissible even if less than five wasaq. There is a hadith: He (Ma'mar) said to him, 'Why did you do that? Go and return it, and take only equal for equal, for I used to hear the Messenger of Allah say: Food for food, equal for equal.' (Reference: Muslim, Bukhari) In this hadith, it is forbidden to sell anything of the same genus, whether measured by weight or capacity, with excess. Therefore, buying dates on the tree for dried dates with excess or deficiency is not permissible, whether it is less than five wasaq or another form of 'araya. Selling weighable or measurable items with excess is not permissible. Buying dates on the tree with something other than dates is permissible, because there is no riba due to difference in genus. [891] (10) The sale by throwing a stone and the sale by touching are not permissible. These were sales from the time of Jahiliyyah. Several items are placed for sale; the buyer throws a stone, and whichever item it hits becomes his, as if offer and acceptance occurred. This is the sale by throwing a stone. The form of malamasah is that several types of merchandise are placed, and the buyer touches one, so that item becomes his; or several buyers are standing, and the seller touches one buyer, making the sale binding on that buyer. Both these sales are invalid. There is deception in both, and it has passed earlier that a sale with deception is not permissible. (2) The hadith forbids both: That Abu Sa'id informed him that the Messenger of Allah forbade munabadhah, which is a man throwing his garment for sale to another before he turns it over or looks at it, and forbade malamasah, which is touching a garment without looking at it. (Reference: Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, munabadhah and malamasah are explained, and the Prophet forbade both. Wherever there is deception about which item is correct and what it is like, its sale is not permissible. All these forms involve gambling, so they are prohibited. [892] (11) And it is not permissible to sell one of two cloths. Footnote: (a) Ma'mar said to him, 'Why did you do that? Go and return it, and take only equal for equal, because I used to hear the Messenger of Allah say: Sell food for food, equal for equal.' (b) He forbade the sale of munabadhah, which is that a man throws his garment for sale to another before he turns it over or looks at it. And he forbade the sale of malamasah, which is that a garment is touched without looking, and the sale becomes binding.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage combines two separate but related rulings: (10) prohibition of sales involving throwing a stone (bay' al-hajar) and touching (bay' al-malamasah), and (11) prohibition of selling one of two cloths without specification (bay' al-thawb min al-thawbayn). The author first reiterates the Hanafi position on muzabanah (from the previous lesson) to reinforce that even small quantities are prohibited due to riba, citing a hadith about exchanging food items equally. Then, the main subject: sales from Jahiliyyah that involve gharar (uncertainty). Bay' al-hajar is where the buyer throws a stone and the item hit is forced upon him. Bay' al-malamasah is where touching an item makes the sale binding. Both are invalid because they lack mutual consent and involve uncertainty. The proof is a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri where the Prophet explicitly forbade munabadhah (similar to throwing) and malamasah. The author notes that these sales involve deception (dhokah), which has been previously established as grounds for prohibition. The third ruling, prohibition of selling one of two cloths without specifying which, is a brief addition: it is not permissible because the object of sale is unspecified. The footnote summarizes the hadith. Overall, the lesson teaches that any contract with significant uncertainty or lack of genuine consent is invalid in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
القاء الحجر (alqa' al-hajar): A sale where the buyer throws a stone at items, and the item hit becomes sold, without inspection or negotiation.
الملامسة (al-malamasah): A sale where simply touching an item or being touched by the seller makes the sale binding, without inspection.
المنابذة (al-munabadhah): A sale where the seller throws the garment to the buyer without allowing inspection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Hanafi school prohibits sales like bay' al-hajar and bay' al-malamasah?
A) Because they involve riba.
B) Because they were from Jahiliyyah and not used by Muslims.
C) Because they involve gharar (uncertainty) and lack of proper consent.
D) Because the goods are not owned by the seller.

Key Takeaway
Any contract where the object of sale is determined by chance, or where consent is not genuine due to uncertainty, is haram and invalid in the Hanafi school.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Prohibition of Sales by Throwing Stones and Touching
Ruling with proof on two forbidden pre-Islamic sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-qa' bi al-hajar and al-mulamasah, explain that the prohibition is due to gharar, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Sales based on chance and deception are void; certainty in transaction terms is mandatory.

This lesson examines two pre-Islamic sales practices that the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly forbade. The Hanafi school holds them void because they involve gharar (uncertainty) and circumvent proper offer and acceptance. Understanding this ruling helps you identify invalid contracts in modern commerce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sales
Note the names: bay' al-qa' bi al-hajar (sale by throwing a stone) and al-mulamasah (sale by touching). Understand their mechanics as explained in the passage.
These are types of deferred transaction with built-in chance.
2
Recognise the element of gharar
Both sales depend on a random outcome (stone hit or touch) rather than mutual consent on a known object. This gharar makes them akin to gambling.
The passage explicitly likens them to gambling (qimar).
3
Cite the hadith proof
The Prophet (ﷺ) prohibited mulamasah and munabadhah. Munabadhah is similar to bay' al-qa' bi al-hajar. The hadith is reported by Abu Sa'id al-Khudri in Bukhari and Muslim.
The prohibition appears as an explicit 'nahy', which in Hanafi usul indicates tahrim.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
Since the act is prohibited (nahy), it is haram. Any sale that includes gharar is invalid (batil) per qiyas on these examples.
Zahir al-riwayah confirms the ruling as batil.

[891] (10) And it is not permissible to sell by throwing a stone and by touching... All these were sales of the time of ignorance. Goods are placed, the buyer throws a stone and it hits one item; that item becomes his, as if offer and acceptance occurred. This is the sale of throwing a stone. The form of touching (mulamasah) is that various items are placed, the buyer touches one, so that sale becomes his; or several buyers are standing, the seller touches one buyer, so that buyer must take the sale. Both sales are invalid. Because both sales involve deception, and it has passed that a deceptive sale is not permissible. (2) The hadith prohibits both sales. That Abu Sa'id informed him that the Messenger of Allah prohibited munabadhah, which is a man throwing his garment for sale to another man before he turns it over or looks at it, and prohibited mulamasah, and mulamasah is touching the garment without looking at it. (Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, munabadhah and mulasamah are explained. And the Prophet prohibited both sales. And where there is deception as to which is correct and how, its sale is not permissible. Gambling contains all these forms, therefore it is unlawful.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on two specific types of prohibited sales: bay' al-qa' bi al-hajar (sale by throwing a stone) and bay' al-mulamasah (sale by touching). The author first states the ruling that these sales are invalid. He then explains the mechanics: in the first, the buyer throws a stone at a pile of goods, and whichever item is hit becomes his; in the second, the buyer touches one of the goods (or the seller touches a buyer) and the sale is concluded. The reasoning is that these sales involve gharar (uncertainty/deception), which has already been established as a prohibition. The proof is a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly forbade munabadhah (similar to throwing a stone) and mulamasah. The author notes that gambling also contains this kind of chance, so it is haram. The Hanafi position is clear: these sales are batil (void) due to the element of gharar. The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim, making the prohibition strong (zahir al-riwayah). The authority weight is from the six core books as the hadith is well-known.

Key Terms
بيع القاء الحجر (bay' al-qa' bi al-hajar): A sale where the buyer throws a stone at goods and whatever the stone hits becomes his.
الملامسة (al-mulamasah): A sale where the buyer touches a piece of cloth (or the seller touches a buyer) without inspection, making the sale binding.
المنابذة (al-munabadhah): A sale where the seller throws his garment to the buyer before the buyer examines it.

Selling One of Two Unspecified Garments: The Hanafi Ruling on Majhool Mabee'
Ruling on the invalidity of selling an unspecified garment from two

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an unspecified garment from two, explain why it is fasid, and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
Selling one of two unspecified garments is fasid due to unknown mabee'; specification in the session makes it valid.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: a person sells one of two different garments without specifying which. The sale is invalid because the object is unknown. However, the defect can be cured if the seller specifies the garment before the session ends. We will explore the reasoning, the principle involved, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling one of two unspecified garments. The ruling in Hanafi fiqh is fasid (voidable), not batil (void).
Fasid is a distinct category: the contract is defective but can be rectified.
2
Locate the reason for invalidity
The reason (illa) is that the object of sale (mabee') is unknown (majhool), leading to potential dispute and falling under the prohibition of gharar.
The prohibition of gharar is established by hadith.
3
Understand the Hanafi concept of fasid
In Hanafi fiqh, fasid means the contract has a defect that can be removed. Unlike batil, which is void ab initio, a fasid contract can become valid if the defect is cured.
The defect here is the ambiguity of the mabee'.
4
Identify the cure: specification within the session
If the seller specifies which garment before the parties separate from the session (majlis), the ambiguity is removed and the sale becomes valid.
This is the only way to rectify the contract; after the session, the contract remains fasid and must be rescinded.
5
Note the cross-madhhab status
Other schools also require the mabee' to be known, but they may classify the contract as batil rather than fasid. The practical difference lies in the possibility of cure.
Hanafi's fasid categorization provides flexibility.

[۸۹۲] (۱۱) اور نہیں جائز ہے دو کپڑوں میں سے ایک کپڑے کی بیع۔ ... دو کپڑے مختلف انداز کے ہیں اور ایجاب کرتے وقت یہ نہیں بتا رہا ہے کہ دونوں میں سے کس کپڑے کی بیع ہورہی ہے، صرف یوں کہہ رہا ہے کہ دونوں کپڑوں میں سے ایک کی بیع ہو رہی ہے تو چونکہ مبیع مجہول ہے بعد میں کپڑا سپرد کرنے میں جھگڑا ہوگا اس لئے یہ بیع فاسد ہوگی۔ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ مجلس ختم ہونے سے پہلے ایک کپڑے کی تعیین ہو جائے تو بیع جائز ہو جائے گی۔ اصول مجہول مبیع کی بیع فاسد ہے۔
[892] (11) And it is not permissible to sell one of two garments. ... The two garments are of different types, and at the time of offer he does not specify which garment is being sold; he only says 'one of the two garments is being sold.' Since the sold item is unknown, there will be dispute later in handing over the garment, therefore this sale is fasid (voidable). The hadith has passed. If before the end of the session the garment is specified, the sale becomes valid. The principle: the sale of an unknown object is fasid.

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: selling one of two unspecified garments is invalid. The reason is that the object of sale (mabee') is majhool (unknown/ambiguous). The author explains that if the seller says 'I sell you one of these two garments' without specifying which, the sale is fasid because it will lead to dispute. However, if the specification occurs within the same session (majlis) before the parties separate, the sale becomes valid because the ambiguity is removed. The proof is implied from the general hadith prohibiting sales of gharar (which has passed earlier in the text). The Hanafi school adheres to the principle that the sale of a majhool mabee' is fasid. This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah. Note: fasid in Hanafi terminology means the sale is voidable—it can be rectified by specification before the session ends.

Key Terms
مبیع مجہول (mabee' majhool): The object of sale that is unknown or unspecified, leading to invalidity.

Selling a Slave with Condition of Manumission
Ruling on sales conditioned with freeing or other acts affecting the slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave with a condition of manumission and classify conditions into the four categories outlined in the passage.

Core Idea
A condition not in harmony with the sale makes it fasid, even if it benefits the slave.

This lesson examines a specific sale where the buyer is required to free the slave or take similar actions. While freeing a slave is virtuous, when it is made a condition of the sale, it introduces an element that is not inherent to the exchange, leading to potential dispute. We will explore why the Hanafi school deems this sale fasid and how conditions are classified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific ruling
State the ruling for each scenario: selling a slave on condition of freeing, making mudabbar, mukatab, or selling a slave-girl on condition of making her umm walad.
The ruling for all is same: sale is fasid.
2
Understand the principle behind the ruling
The principle is that a condition not in harmony with the sale (mukhalif li-muqtada al-'aqd) renders the sale fasid. Recognize why such conditions are problematic: they create uncertainty and potential conflict.
Harmony means the condition is required by the sale itself, like delivery.
3
Classify the four types of conditions
Memorize the four categories: (1) conditions inherent in sale (valid), (2) conditions benefiting seller contrary to sale (invalid), (3) conditions benefiting buyer contrary to sale (invalid), (4) conditions benefiting the object of sale (invalid if object is human and can dispute).
The case at hand falls under type 4.
4
Analyze the hadith proofs
Two hadiths are cited: (a) any condition not from Allah's Book is batil, (b) no two conditions in one sale. Understand how these support the ruling that such conditions invalidate the sale.
The first hadith is general; the second specifically prohibits two conditions.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that such conditions are void but the sale remains valid. Appreciate the practical difference: in Hanafi, the sale is fasid and must be rectified.
This difference stems from how each school views the linkage between condition and contract.

بنائے گا تو بیع فاسد ہے۔ یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ بیچ کے ساتھ ایسی شرط لگائی جو بیچ کے موافق نہیں ہے تو وہ بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ شرط لگانے کی چار صورتیں ہیں (۱) ایسی شرط جو خود بیج کے موافق ہے، مثلا بیج اس لئے کرتا ہوں کہ مشتری کا قبضہ ہو جائے تو شرط خود بیع کا مقتضاء ہے اس لئے اس سے بیع فاسد نہیں ہوگی (۲) ایسی شرط لگائی جو بیع کے موافق نہیں ہے البتہ بائع کا فائدہ ہے، مثلا اس شرط پر گھر بیچتا ہوں کہ دو ماہ تک اس میں رہوں گا۔ اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی (۳) اس شرط پر بیع کرے کہ مشتری کا فائدہ ہو مثلا اس شرط پر خریدتا ہوں کہ مجھے مزید قرض دیں گے۔ اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی (۴) اور شرط کی چوتھی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایسی شرط لگائے جس میں خود مبیع کا فائدہ ہو اور بیع غلام یا باندی ہو جو قاضی کے قضا تک پہنچ سکتا ہو۔ اس صورت میں مبیع کا فائدہ ہے اور طبع جھگڑا کر سکتا ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں بھی ہے شریعت کے خلاف جو بھی شرط لگائے اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے (۲) دوسری حدیث میں ہے عبد الله بن عمر قال قال رسول الله الله لا يحل سلف و بيع ولا شرطان في بيع (ب) (ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی الرجل بیچ مالیس عندہ ج ثانی ص ۱۳۹ نمبر ۳۵۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ دوشرطیں لگانا ممنوع ہے۔ اور خاص طور پر جس شرط لگانے سے جھگڑے کا خطرہ ہو اس سے بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
[893] (12) And whoever sells a slave on condition that the buyer frees him, or makes him mudabbar, or makes him mukatab, or sells a slave-girl on condition that he makes her umm walad, then the sale is fasid. These issues are based on the principle that if a condition is attached to a sale that is not in harmony with the sale, the sale becomes fasid. There are four forms of conditions: (1) A condition that is in harmony with the sale itself, e.g., I sell so that the buyer takes possession; this condition is required by the sale, so it does not make it fasid. (2) A condition that is not in harmony with the sale but benefits the seller, e.g., I sell this house on condition that I live in it for two months; in this case the sale is fasid. (3) A condition that benefits the buyer, e.g., I buy on condition that you give me an additional loan; this makes the sale fasid. (4) The fourth type is a condition that benefits the object of sale itself (the slave) and the object is a slave who can reach the qadi's judgment; in this case it benefits the object and can lead to dispute, so the sale is fasid. The above case falls under this fourth type because freeing, making mudabbar, or mukatab benefits the slave, and since the object is a human, he can complain, so the sale is fasid. The hadith prohibits attaching conditions that contradict the Shari'ah: from A'ishah... the Prophet said: 'What is the matter with people who stipulate conditions that are not in the Book of Allah? Any condition not in the Book of Allah is batil, even if it is a hundred conditions. The decree of Allah is more worthy and the condition of Allah is more binding.' (Bukhari, Muslim) Another hadith: 'It is not lawful to combine a loan and a sale, nor two conditions in one sale.' (Abu Dawud)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses a complex fiqhi issue: selling a slave (or slave-girl) with a condition that the buyer performs an act that ultimately benefits the slave, such as freeing him, making him mudabbar (free upon the owner's death), mukatab (contract for freedom), or making a female slave umm walad (free upon the owner's death after bearing a child). The author rules that such a sale is fasid (voidable) because the condition is not in harmony with the sale itself. The passage then outlines a general classification of conditions in sales: (1) conditions that are inherent in the sale (like delivery) are valid; (2) conditions beneficial to the seller contrary to the sale's nature are invalid; (3) conditions beneficial to the buyer are invalid; (4) conditions beneficial to the object of sale (especially when the object is a human) are invalid because they can cause dispute. The case at hand falls into the fourth category: freeing the slave benefits him, and since he is a rational being, he may demand it, leading to conflict. The proof is from two hadiths: the first from A'ishah (RA) stating that conditions contrary to Allah's Book are void, and the second from Ibn Umar (RA) prohibiting two conditions in one sale. The Hanafi school holds this as fasid (not batil) because the condition is void but the sale may be rectified by removing the condition. However, the passage states the sale is fasid outright. Authority weight: the hadiths are from the Six Books, so this is from the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the owner's death.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract to purchase freedom by paying installments.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her owner; she becomes free upon his death.
شرط (shart): A condition attached to a contract. Conditions are classified into four types in this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because freeing slaves is discouraged
B) Because it benefits the seller
C) Because it benefits the slave and can cause dispute
D) Because it is a condition that is against the Shari'ah

Key Takeaway
A sale with a condition that benefits the object of sale and creates potential dispute is fasid in the Hanafi school.

Conditions That Invalidate a Sale: The Four Types and Their Proofs
Ruling and classification of conditions in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of conditions in sales according to the Hanafi school, identify which conditions invalidate the sale, and explain the Hanafi proof against such conditions.

Core Idea
A condition contrary to the nature of a sale invalidates it, even if beneficial to the object.

In this passage from Al-Sharh al-Samari, the author expands on one of the most important principles in Hanafi sales law: the classification of conditions attached to sales. You will learn that not all agreed-upon conditions are valid—some can render a sale fasid (voidable). The author presents a four-part typology, supported by hadith and a critical response to the Shafi'i position. By understanding this, you will be able to distinguish valid trade practices from those that introduce gharar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and principle
The core ruling is that a sale with a condition contrary to its nature (muqawim) is fasid. The principle is that the condition must be consistent with the essence of the sale.
The term fasid means voidable, not void ab initio (batil).
2
Recognize the four types of conditions
List the four categories: (1) consistent with the sale (valid), (2) contrary but beneficial to seller (fasid), (3) contrary but beneficial to buyer (fasid), (4) contrary but beneficial to the object itself when the object is human (fasid).
Types 2,3,4 all lead to fasid because the condition is extraneous.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet (saw) said: 'Any condition not in the Book of Allah is invalid, even if there are a hundred conditions.' This general prohibition supports the Hanafi position that such conditions render the sale invalid.
The hadith is from Aisha (Bukhari, Muslim) and is used as a primary proof.
4
Understand the Shafi'i counter-argument
Imam al-Shafi'i cites the incident of Barirah, where Aisha bought a slave-girl with the condition of freeing her, and the Prophet did not object. This suggests that conditions benefiting the object are valid.
The Shafi'i school sees this as an exception or evidence of permissibility.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes the Barirah case: the condition of freeing is for a religious benefit, not a commercial one, and the slave is capable of litigation. The general prohibition remains decisive.
The hadith of Jabir (riding the camel) is also discussed as an exception for temporary usufruct.

Source Text
